O you, three dear sons, I have completed this book which is the end of the science of medicine together with its fulfillment, and I have made the end through its explanations and through its declarations. And it came to me in a vision that I complete it with this treatise, which is part of the work of hands, that is to say "surgery".
And because the work of hands is fortified in our region and is strange everywhere, so has the science perished and been cut off, and nothing remains of its force except small descriptions in books of the ancients that the hands have moved. And from this have come error and vanity, and are closed the understandings and is alienated the science.
Or it seems to me that I bring it to life together with the arrangement of this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and I will bring together the forms of the instruments of the cautery, and the other instruments of the work with the adjustment of the declaration and preparation of them, as it is necessary.
And for the reason of those that no good masters of hands are found in our time, the art of medicine is elongated. And it is necessary for him who undertakes this to climb in the science of anatomy, as Galien says, until he knows the judgment of the members, and the forms and conjunctions and separations of joints, and have knowledge of the bones and nerves and veins and the number of vessels, and the movement and of moving and resting veins, and their place of exit.
And for this Hippocrates says that the name with the name is much, and with the work little; and similarly in the art of the hand.
We say to you at the beginning of this book that whoever is not acquainted with the things said before, if he does not beware lest he fall into error and harm men;
in such a way that I have seen many times more of those who interfere in this science and abandon themselves to their ignorance and lack of experiment.
For I see a fool look at an abscess of scrofulous or the neck of a woman, and cuts the arteries in the neck. For because so much blood came out that the woman fell dead in his hands.
And I see another doctor treating a stone from a man's body, and the stone was large; so he took out with it a piece of the bladder, and the man died within three days.
And I have already been called for this treatment, and I see the disposition of the patient and the size of the stone, and well if one cut it they would die.
And I see another doctor taking care of the Duke of our land who had a broken leg, and with the break there was a wound;
and the doctor, full of ignorance, forced the broken bone onto the wound, and did not put any lint on the wound, and left it like that.
and he commanded him not to unbind it at all;
and so remained until the leg was all swollen and the foot and came to ruin.
I am called to come to him, and I hastened to come to untie the leg; and when I heard it dislocate, it was in peace and felt no pain.
But by all means the corruption had already been confirmed in the member, and I could not heal it;
and did not cease the corruption to go through the member until the member was entirely lost.
And see another kind which breaks open the abscess and then inflames by the evil of its lordship.
For a sore, when it is pure of melancholic humor, it is not proper that one touch it with iron unless in such a place where one can remove all that is corrupted.
And therefore, dear sons, it is necessary that you know that the work of hands is divided into II divisions: that is to say, works where there is danger in many positions;
and I will show in each place of this book where there is fear and peril.
For which reason it is necessary that you keep guard, so that the foolish ones may not speak badly of you.
If you take your arms in caution, and undertake your patients lightly and firmly, and use the best way so that by the grace of God you come to salvation and be praised;
and leave the hopeful diseases to good health;
and remove your arms from those who make you doubt, and have done in yourselves so that you may come from this world to the other of glory.
Galians says in some of his admonitions: Do not take care of any bad illness, lest you be called a foolish one."
I have devised this treatise according to three chapters.
The first chapters are of fire cauteries and cauteries of medicine sharp, and are the chapters ordered from the head to the feet;
and the shapes of the instruments, and the tools of cautery, and all that is necessary in the work.
The second chapter is of cutting and probing, and of suction cups and wounds, and the extraction of arrows, and similar things;
and this chapter orders and the shapes of the instruments.
The third chapter is of restoring dislocations and twists, and similar things;
and it is ordered from the head to the foot, and the shapes of the instruments.
Thus as we remember from the work, it is necessary that I say the quality of the aid and of the annoyance of each complexion.
I say that the qualities of aid, and of nuisance, are the longest speech and subtle sciences and the secret answers;
and of this speak most sages, and agree to this;
and I suffice few their words, because I fear the length.
I say that cautery helps universally to every complexion or it has matter, except for if there are two complexions. That is to say, hot complexion without matter, and dry complexion without matter.
In a hot and dry complexion together with matter, some have said and declared that cautery there is great help; and the others say the opposite. That cautery is not worth for disease that comes from heat and dryness. Because the nature of fire is so hot, and it should not be cured for a hot disease by heat, nor by dryness;
and he who says the opposite states that cautery does great help to hot and dry diseases that come in the bodies of men. For when you examine the body of man and his moisture to the complexion of fire, you find the body cold.
And I agree to this word. For I have found it never by experiment.
And in any case, do not interfere unless he who has proven it and knows by long usage the chapter of cauteries, and the diversity of complexions of men, and the dispositions and causes of their diseases, and the appearances and space of time.
Of other complexions we have no more fear, even in cold and wet diseases. And all the masters assembled and agreed on the cautery.
And know, sons, that fire cautery together with secrets of the treatments and more good than cautery of burning medicine.
For the heat is simple, and does not come forth except to the member which is burnt, and does not harm another member besides that one.
The ardent medicines come from member to member by removing, and bring to the member diseases of bad healing. And perhaps it harms the patient.
The fire for its height and for the goodness of its substance does not do this, unless there is superfluity.
And this I have found by experience and the length of service and study that I have put into the art, and was based on the truth of things;
and for this I excuse myself from the length of the speech, because it does not please me that I keep from you the secret which is contained in fire, and the quality of the work, how it cures diseases of the body, as the philosopher says in demonstrating. or receive it in your thoughts.
And know, son, that it is agreed upon in what time cauteries should be made, and how long they are to be applied.
And I say that cautery is suitable at all times, for this reason that the harms coming are diminished with the help of cautery; and also if the cauteries are for necessary pains before and until they cannot await the time that greater evil does not come to the limb.
And do not ever think, dear child, as the foolish ones do; because to the illness which is cured by cautery has no return; and they say thus;
and they say thus; but it is not as they think, because cauteries are not just medicines that change the complexion, and dry up the humors that are the cause of pain, and are more valuable than medicine for the speed of its effect, and for the virtue of its work and of its power.
And a most important thing is that the disease often comes according to the complexion of the sick person and the place of the disease and its power.
And these excesses are assembled in him by the acquisition of meats and similar things, if it is not cauteries administered where there are few excesses and moisture, such as cauteries of fire for pain, and similar things, and such pain does not return. but this happens rarely.
And many people do not understand why cautery is called the last resort of medicine, and it is true.
but they do not believe that cautery is worth after medicine, but their intentions are false, and it is cauteries the last of medicine for those when we administer manners of cures in any disease and they do not work, then we administer our cautery, and it works.
Therefore it is clear that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the intention of foolish people.
The ancients said that cautery of an instrument of gold is more effective than of iron, and they said this those who were learned because of the quality of gold and the height of its substance;
and they said that the lesions of cautery do not cause rot, but this is not true because I have proven it, and I have found that it is true in some men;
and cautery of gold is worth more than iron, as they said. And anyway when you heat an instrument of gold, you do not know when it is heated by the color of the gold, and it is soon cooled;
and if one heats it too much; if done to fire; and thus there is enough impediment.
Or I have ordered this chapter of the head down to the feet, so that the path may be easier to find.
This cautery is good for moisture and cold on the head, and there are two ways to make it by reason of a disease called "soda," and because of many humors that descend from the head as the eyes, and ears, and because of a multitude of dreams and toothache and sore throat, and also all diseases that come from cold such as paralysis and stroke and apoplexy and similar diseases.
The form of this cautery is that the patient be purged by a medicinal solution modifying the head III or IV, according to what the virtues and the age and the custom of the patient can bear. and then make an opening on the head of the patient.
And then make him sit crossed between your hands, and put his hands on your lap;
after put the root of your palm on the root of his nose between his two eyes; and where your two fingers come together, if it bleeds then cauterize with the tool called 'olivare', because this is its form: And put it on the spot marked with ink, and make as you descend and withdraw your hand slightly pulling back and forth turning it; Then gently move your hand slightly, and look at the place if you see the bone small. And if you do not see it, then restart the cautery until you see the bone as I have told you.
After that take a piece of silk, and put in it soft and moist cotton, and put it on the place, and let it there for 3 days.
After that put on moist cotton in butter. And let it there until the burning is gone. After that treat the wound with ointment called "tetrafarmaçon", until it is healed.
And they say the ancients, that when the wound remains open longer, the more it discharges. And for this reason, they help to move it.
And they said any of these that one must let the cautery so long that part of the bone is burned, and then sew up what is burned of the bone, and be cured.
And they say others that one should leave the cautery on so long, and put it down so strongly that one lowers the bone as wide as the whorl that women put in their spindles for those that the humors might come out moved.
and one must leave the wound long open; afterwards one must cure and dress it.
And these two manners of cauteries do not seem to me to be good at all except in certain men where there is a way of fear;
and I seem to myself that I should leave him;
for the shoulder is weakened by continued solutions of nature, as we testify in other members;
and even when the shoulder of the patient is weak by nature, and the first appearance of cautery is more salutary and effective for me, and from this I operate in thus as a whole health.
When throughout the head comes chronic pain, and lasts for a long time, then the patient should use herbs and pills which are called "cochie," and from purgation of the head and oils and plasters; and if one does to him the cautery that we have said before, and it is not effective. Take ward so that the shoulder of the sick person is of strong nature, and that he is not too cold; Therefore, cauterize there below with that cautery we have said above. After do cautery on each horn of the head up to the bone, as we have said before, and do cautery where it is called "vessels"; and put there those where one puts cupping glasses; and alight your hand so that you cannot see up to the bone: The patient feels much greater pain in this cautery than others, and I will speak of it later;
And it is necessary that the cauteries of the horns be more subtle than those with which one cauterizes the middle of the head, and this is the form:
of cautery of the head When in one part of the head comes sudden pain, and it extends to the eye, and the patient is purged by medicinal fumigations, and one has made the other cures that I have said in the division of the illnesses, and all of these do not help: Therefore I do cauteries according to two manners, that is to say of hot medicine and of fire.
The cauteries of hot medicine are such that you take a piece of lead and remove the rind and cut it into two pieces as big as your thumb, and then put the temple where the pain holds in such manner that you can replace the piece of lead under the skin.
And when it will be replaced, so tie and hold it well and leave it thus for twelve hours. and then untie it and treat the wound with lead, and leave the wound thus for two or three days. Then put a poultice soaked in butter on top until it heals.
After the cure of ointment until it is healed.
And if you see such things done of certain medicines burning which I have written or do as I have treated burning medicines.
The iron cautery is done this way. Heat the cautery of which here is the form:
And is called "closed" because the head is made in the shape of a clasp or it has the point of curvature, and in the middle a small appearance.
And put it on the painful place, and hold your hand and touch the iron lightly a little, until half of the thickness of the heart is burned.
And lift your hand so that the artery does not burn, which is outside, and flows an abundance of blood.
After put cotton in a wad of silk, and put it on the place for three days. then put cotton moistened in butter, and then treat the place with ointment until it is healed.
And if you want to cauterize this migraine with the cautery called "cultellare," at its whole point, and keep from covering the artery in this migraine which is not ancient.
of migraine
When you treat migraine as we have said before in the divisions of illnesses, and it does not profit, and see that the cauteries before said of the medicine and of iron do not help him, then it is necessary that you heat the cautery which is called "cultellare" until it is white;
and then reign so on the place ailing a line of half a finger of long, and remove your hand once, and strain that you do not cut the artery and that you do not come up to the bone;
and guard the jaw that it moves when one brings it up, and the nerve is not looked at except by the movement and that spasm does not come to the patient, and guard against loss of blood from the artery when it will be cut, or cutting has great fear and even for him who does not know about it, and who is not studying and experienced; and let the work be halfway done;
But I will tell you how to restrain blood when the artery is cut, and I will show you where it appears in this book.
And if you see that this cautery does not suffice to the sickness, and the patient is suffering, cauterize the middle as we have said, and cure the wound until it is healed;
And if you see, administer the cautery that we have said in the chapter of the extraction of the artery, with the cautery that has two blades; and this cautery is better than that one and more helpful.
of the pain of the ears from cold

After, prick all around the ear of the sick person, or where the pain is felt in both, and move it a little above the root of the ear once you have marked the place to cut. and there are 10 points around each ear;
after curing the place until it is healthy.
of the mouth's torture

and put the cautery in the part that opens healthy, and make cautery at the top of the ear above a finger below the horn of the head, and another one in the temple as long as a finger; and let the cautery remain there as long as the middle of the heart is burned

of lethargy

and this is the manner of the cautery: if you do it as I have said.

of epilepsy

The hair that pricks the eye Sometimes it happens that the hairs of the eyes are converted to the eyelids, and prick the eye, and one does cauteries according to II manners: that is to say of fire, or of burning medicine.
For the fire cautery, instruct the patient to let the hairs grow long and even; and if they prick at their base, hold the eyes steady so they do not move until they grow.
When they are born and are equal, then place the patient in your healthy condition; after draw on the paper of the eyes a figure that is as like as the leaf of myrtle, and let it be the beginning of the sign near the hairs;
then put cotton moistened in egg white and  a bit of persimmon over the eye, then heat the cautery as follows:
After that cauterize on the figure that you have signed here and there many times, so that the hairs are burned in that place.
and the sign of the health of the work is that you see the paper retreated, and the hairs raised to the height of the eye.
Therefore lift your hand, and leave it so for III days, then put moistened cotton in butter until the burn is gone; after the ointment treatment until it is healed.
And if after this the hairs come, and the skin is softened then begin the cautery again on that same place, as you did first;
and if the hairs are cut off, cauterize them until the skin is raised to its natural place, and it be even, and do not prick the hair in the eye.
The cautery of burning medicine is that you command the patient to let the hairs grow long and even, then make a shape of parchment like the leaf of myrtle.
then take French soap and quicklime, of each a third, and grind them together well; and make it hastily so that it does not cool, then spread it on the shape you have made like the leaf of myrtle, and put it over the eyes of the patient. and if the patient holds in one eye only, then you must work in that one, and if in both eyes as well. and put under the eye cotton moistened in vinegar.
and the shoulder of the sick person be in your healthy and put your knee showing on the medicine, and press it and its point all times that the sick person feels burning from the medicine, because he feels burning as well as fire.
When you have done this for a long time, then remove the medicine to your place. And when the burning will be passed, so lift the place of awe, and reward yourself if you see the parchment raised as if it had been raised by constraint of fire or by incision; and if it is so, then it is well.
If not so, so put back more of the medicine on the place where the medicine did not work, as long as your works are equal, and your eyes set to point;
then put on cotton moistened in butter as long as the wound is wet, after cure the place of the ointment which is called 'diaphenis' and other, as long as it is healthy.
And if it is necessary that you guard well your work with great care so that no medicine falls into the eyes.
And if the paper becomes soft afterward, and the hair pricks in the eye, then begin again the medicine on that part which is softened by the paper properly, just as you did before; after curing it until it is healed;
and this is the form of the leaf of the myrtle:
And know that the eyes of men are diverse in size and smallness, and according to these be your constrictions;
and let not the right way be returned to him whose study is this art.
of the cure of fistula by cautery
When you cure a fistula as we have said in the divisions of diseases, and your cures do not benefit, then it is necessary that you cauterize it according to this manner.
Instruct the patient to put his head on your healthy side; and hold the head between your hands in such a manner that it does not move or tilt its head;
after put moistened cotton in water of an egg or in the juice of parsley on the eye;
After heating the cautery which is as follows:
and is shaped at the head like the form of a quill feather, altogether so that it is the cautery;
if you wish, it shall be pointed from one head to the other, and if you wish, it shall also be like a tent;
but nonetheless, this which is shaped is more useful for your work.
After press the fistula if it is open, and dry out its matter and if it is closed, so the work, and extract fiercely the core.
After put upon the hot heated cautery form, and hold your hand on the cautery at the part where it touches above the eye, so that your hands do not make a mistake. and place the cautery in the fat of the eye, and it corrodes.
If you come to the beginning of the cautery up to the bone, it would be good. If this is not done, restart the cautery again once, and another if you have need of it, and leave it for 3 days.
afterward put on moistened cotton in butter, and treat with the ointment of desiccation until it is healed, and if 40 days pass and it is not healed, then put on an acrid corrosive medicine until the bone is uncovered; and treat according to what comes in this chapter.
And there is a way of cauterizing a fistula that none of the ancients said. Attend a fistula, and open its place; afterward put in the fissure one subtle camomile of which this is the form: and make it to the quantity of 1/3 of a plum ; and hold your hand or camomile firmly, and let the patient not move at all so that the melted plums do not run to his eye ;
and it is necessary to put on the eye of the patient cotton moistened in wine or ale:
The pimple if it is at its place in a fistula, and the healthy one by miraculous healing.
And if the fistula is healed by the cautery that we have said, it is good. If this is not the case, it is necessary to administer purging to the nostril and bring the fistula back to the body of the nostril according to what is proper in its own place.
of the openings in lips Many times there are wounds in lips which are called “hair”, and even in children’s lips.

and do thus once or two, after the cure of what we have said in treatments, until he is healed.
and if he is healed thus, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to uncover the place and remove the corrupted bone, according to what you will find in your chapter.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth move, and you treat it with medicines and it is not effective, then put the head of the sick person in your hand; after heating the cautery whose form will come here after in such a manner that you put a handle on the tooth;
and put inside the heated cautery, and hold it so long that the patient feels the heat coming to the root of the tooth;
after lightly touch your hand and begin the cautery according to your will
after fill the patient's mouth with salt water, and let it stay there for an hour and spit forcefully.
And in such manner the tooth that was moving is thus confirmed, and the softened gum restrained, and the moisture corrupted dry.
of the pain of teeth
When tooth pain comes from cold, or there is cold or dampness, and the treatments of medicine are not helpful, then there is need for cauterization according to two ways: and cautery of butter and cautery of fire.
Cauterization of butter is such that you take butter from a churn and the end in a spoon of iron or steel; after take cotton and wrap it on the point of a needle, and moisten it in melted butter, and put it on the aching tooth gently and hold it until it is cooled.
and thus do often until its virtue reaches the root of the tooth, and if you see so moistened wool and cotton in butter cold, put it on the aching tooth, and place a hot iron above it until the heat comes to the end of the tooth.
The cautery by fire is such that you take a chain of iron or steel, and it should be thick so that the flame does not come to the mouth of the sick person.
After heating the cautery, according to its form which will come later, and place it on the tooth and hold your hand until the cautery is cooled.
Do this often, because the pain will be removed that same day or the next.
And it is necessary that the patient fill his mouth with good butter at the time of this cautery, and then rinse it.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And make a cautery of whatever shape you wish, and according to what you are able.
And this is the form of the chain:

For the cautery of pleurisy
The ancients said that one must do cauteries with the roots of aristolochia and in such manner: Take a root of aristolochia long, the largest that you will be able to find, and whether it is dry, and as thick and as long as your thumbs. and moisten it in oil, and imprint it in the fire, and cauterize with this cautery between the shoulder and the neck, and make small cauteries one by one descending in the part that is below the beard.
And make two cauteries between the fifth rib and the sixth, declining behind a little. And with the cautery remove the pain, and the other on the stomach, and between the two shoulders, and the two ribs of the side where the cautery is between the shoulders.
And do not press your hand at all, but make it open as if smelling.
And they say that no one used a cautery of fire resembling a razor, and heat it and put it between the ribs, until it comes to this abscess. And jumps outside the pain, as we have said in the abscess of the liver.
But in this work there is fear where the patient dies now, or a fistula comes which cannot be healed.

The cure of a chancre by cautery
When an ulcer comes at the beginning, and you see it to be arrested, cauterize it with a cautery that seems like a circle, all around the perimeter of the ulcer.
And nevertheless, the sages say that one should cauterize also in the middle of the ulcer;
and nevertheless these do not seem good to me, because I fear that it may become ulcerated and corrupted, and I have never wanted this to happen before.
and a more skillful thing is that it be cauterized with the cautery called "circle," as we have said among many cauteries.
When someone has an ailment called "adubelati," and it delays in dying either because of the superfluity it produces or because of the blood of the patient when he is decrepit (that is to say too old) and has little blood, or because of the tension; and you see it hasten to die: then cauterize it around with many small cauteries in pricking. afterwards leave it so. For she will hasten to die.
And if you see it necessary to perform the whole cautery, then heat the cautery with this form:
And put your hand in the middle of the illness that is called “adubelati” until the cautery has pierced the heart, and let the cautery be such that the patient feels it burning in such a way that the course of the rot is alleviated.
After the cure as long as it is perfectly healed.
One other way of sickness there is called 'corrodens', and it is nothing else than a gnawing of the limb, because it wastes the limbs as if the lines were dried up.
If you see this illness in a place where it can endure cauterization with fire all at once, then sharpen the cautery, small and large, according to what is fitting for the place where the illness is;
After the cautery of all parts as long as you reach all the corruption, and nothing remains of it, that all that is corrupted be removed.
And then let him be for III days, and put over the cauterized places softened broth with oil until the scabs of fire are removed, and all the corruption.
After the cure of suppurations causing flesh.
And if you see after three days that healthy flesh does not grow, it is good.
And if this is not the case, begin the cautery again on that which remains corrupted.
And this malady has never been cured by medicine alone instead of fire cautery;
But nonetheless, fire cautery is of greater help, and I have already said the cure of medicinal herbs in the division: Take it when you need it.
of the sprouts which come to the feet Many times it happens that the feet by fire give birth to a sickness which is called “closed” according to our understanding, and it is a large thing fouled which blunts the foot.
And the cautery for this malady is according to 2 manners. This is to know fire cautery, and cautery of hot water.
Fire cautery is that you heat the iron cautery as it is a vulture's feather, and it should be made of good iron to the measure that it can reach the nail in all parts, and that it has subtle margins;
After place it all hot upon the nail, and turn your hand all around the nail until it comes to the bottom of the nail. And let it thus for III days until it begins to get rot; Then make it an ointment of wild mallows bruised with oil, and leave the ointment upon all night, and the nail will fall and be uprooted from its roots.
Then cure the place with ointment closing flesh until it is healed.
And if they are converted by fire the body, and many times happen in hands and feet, then it is necessary that you bring them a cautery of hot water; and is in such manner: Take a cannon of iron or steel, or a feather of vulture, and put upon the nail and the althelul, that is to say corns, warts; and put the cannon upon it so that it pricks all around, then put a drop of hot oil, and hold your hand well, and press your hand turning so that the edge of the cannon comes to the root of the nail, and can find the hot oil going deep until the root;
and lets the patient suffer a little the sting of hot water an hour; then lets him so, and it will dislodge itself from all its roots.
Thus do one after another, until you have scraped all those that are on the body, then cure the wounds that engender flesh.
When some shiver from cold, or from quartan fever, or another, it is necessary that you make him four cauteries or four on the spine of his back, one cautery between each vertebra. and one cautery or weight, and one on the stomach at the olive cautery;
so letting him tremble, and will hasten his health from the cold disease.
At no time appear by fires the bathed body, or puss-filled pustules, that come from large corrupted materials.
it is necessary at first when they appear that one makes a cautery on each head of the pustule: a subtle cautery of myrtle stick, so that its point is seized by fire, or of long birthwort root, or the lentil cautery.
And sometimes when they appear at the beginning, they are cauterized in this manner, and they cannot grow back from then on, but the superfluities that come from them are removed, and they are healed.
But it is necessary that this be done after the evacuation of blood.
Many times great hemorrhages of blood occur from the artery that is cut at the wound which comes to the outside, or in the incision of an abscess, or in the cauterization of the limb, and similar things. And it is a very grave thing to prolong.
And yet when this happens to anyone, hasten to place your index finger on the wound of the vein, and restrain it very much until the bleeding is appeased beneath your finger, and no more drops issue.
then put on the fire olive cautery more small and large as they are all red;
then take it [the cautery], whether large or small according to the wound and its place where the artery is cut, and put the cautery on that vein where you hold your hand, and hold the cautery as long as the blood is stopped.
And if it jumps out when you lift your arm above the vein, and the cautery is extinguished, then take hastily another cautery from the fire.
And do not cease to make those one after another until the bleeding is stopped.
and guard that you do not burn the nerve which is nearby, and that no other harm comes to the patient.
And know that when without cutting of the artery, one cannot well stitch it even when the artery is large, unless by one of the four ways that we have said: or by cautery, or by extraction when it is not treated, or by strong tying of thread, or by medicines put on that have power to stitch blood. And be bound and strained well.

And if this happens to anyone and there is no medicine present, then one must put his two index fingers on the wound of the vein as we have said, and well strain until it is stitched above the wound and above the vein. and he must not move them, but often moisten them with cold water that is very cold until the blood is congealed and thickened and stitched.
And make inquiries beforehand of those who need cautery and medicines.
We have told you in the first chapter all illnesses where it is necessary to use fire cautery, or hot medicines, and their origins and causes and instruments and forms of the cauteries;
And we have placed this chapter from the head down to the feet, and in this chapter I will follow the same way for those things that are found more easily for those who want them.
And thus as I begin these, it is necessary, dear child, that you learn that in this chapter there is more danger and fear than in the first of cauteries, and for that reason greater caution is needed.
For in the work of this chapter there is often the evacuation of blood when opening a vein, and incising an abscess, and in the treatment of wounds, and in the extraction of arrows, and in cutting into stones, and similar things; where there is doubt and fear, and many die.
And I warn you that you do not undertake folly. For many kinds of people come to cures. If they come from such ones who have suffered so much with their sickness that they die easily because of the famine they find in their illness, and because of the greatness of their wickedness. And from these one should have fear.
And of these there is one who gives you his meaning, and says: Wait, I am healed"; and the sickness is spiteful.
It is not fitting that you consent to those who come to you who have this manner, but put more emphasis on your honor than on your greed, and do not open yourself fully but wisely to those of whom you are certain, and from whom you can take and profit.
and use prognostication and indication in the treatment of your patients so that health may be restored.

of the stopping up of the ears

of the rot of the eye When this sickness which is called "aleacrati" or the rotting comes into the eye, there are two ways of it.
one natural, and the other unnatural.
And it happens by a sudden blow from a wound or incision or cautery, and by similar things.

Now I will tell you the two proper ways of suturing:
Take the needle, and thrust it from the outside part to the inside.
When it has pierced the right heart straight through, then lay the head on the side of the scalp where you thrust the needle, and make it pierce from the other side from the inside to the outside.
And the understanding of this suture is that you sew as the furrier does furs, and that you leave the scalp on one side, and take it back on the other, until the suture is entirely completed.
The second manner of suturing, as Galens states properly, is like any one who joins the two parts together naturally, and applies one side of the scalp to the other side, in such a way:
Insert a needle or head of the scalpel near it, and make it pierce only that place, and leave the two sides of the scalp. Then turn the needle, and make it pierce from the outside part until the inside one, just like two shoulders.
Then make it pierce both from the outside part until the inside one, in two other shoulders of the miracle that are on the other side.
And this is the most common and usual way to suture.
The Gauls say this word thus, and no one testifies it, it is necessary to sew the belly with two needles, or there is thread in each.

and I will tell you of a fistula which happened to the man in his leg, so that you may take example from it and help in your work.
He was a young man around thirty years old, who had pain in his leg from a cause that came from within the body, so that the matter came to the leg, and it was swollen with a large abscess, and there was no external cause for opening;
but the wounds by their folly work so much that the abscesses open and they corrupt much of matter, and become fistula, and there came much pus that all lying bowed and moist from the body;
and many wounds they did care for over II years, and there was no wise man in the art of hands, until I came to them called, and I saw his leg and the medicines with which they were treating him, and what corrupted by pus to great corruption; and becoming red the body of the youth, and had a color of citron.
I set up a tent in one of these pits, and then searched all the pits, and found that some came against each other from all parts of the leg.
I therefore hastened, and dug into one of the pits until I discovered a part of the bone, and found it rotten and pierced, and the corruption came up to the inside bone.
I was certain that what appeared to me of the rotten bone, and I thought there was no other corruption, except that which I had removed;
and began to restore the wound with hot medicinal poultices for a long time, and could not heal it;
And then I started again, and discovered the bone as before, and found the corruption continued in the bone, and removed it, and then tried to solder the place; but I could not heal until I dug around a palm's breadth of the bone, and found it entirely rotten. Then I restored it with medicines; And he was healed and joined soundly, and it did not again become necessary to begin the work except for the weakness of the patient, who could not endure, and for the fear of death that I had of him. For the evacuation of blood often occurred, but it was cured completely, and in place of the bone grew a hard cartilage, and it was well-disposed and did not have any inconvenience or contrary at all, neither going nor coming.
And if the bone is broken in any place of the body, then it should be treated in this manner: Take a linen cloth and strain it at the head of the bone that is open, and command any one to extend it upwards. And put another linen cloth, thicker than the first one, and tie it over the skin that is above the bone, and extend the head upwards so that you pull the corrupted flesh downwards, and uncover the flesh in the place where you wish to be, so that the bone does not bleach the flesh;

Here begins the book of fractures This chapter is many times necessary in the art of medicine, and is restoration of fracture and dislocation.
Know this, dear son, that the fools who are looking to this chapter, will never have seen any of the ancient books, nor read a letter from them.
And for this reason, science is estranged in our land, because I never found any good one.
And I know nothing of it, except through the science of ancient books, which I have studied so much that I have mastered its science, and by experience, and by the custom of my life.
And I have shown you in this chapter what is contained, and abridged it at the end, and clearly showed the forms of the instruments that are brought, as I did in these two chapters before.
It is therefore necessary that we say or prologue of this book the sums of the word and the chapters where the sages put their understanding, and remain on the truth, so that those who are experienced know and learn this noble art, and learn it to others.
I say that when a fracture or dislocation or torsion or sprain comes to anyone, it is necessary that you hasten to heal the patient on one side and the other, if nothing hinders you, such as weakness of body, or infancy, or old age; or if the heat is not very good, or too cold;
and eat cold meats, that is to say, cold mutton and venison, and do not drink wine, nor eat any fatty meats, and do not fill yourself too much with meats that fill the veins with blood, as long as you have doubt of a hot abscess, or an expansion of matter in the limb;
If the patient returns to his former regimen that he has used.
And when the broken bone begins to heal, it is necessary that one nourishes the patient with meats that make a thick nourishment, or there is viscosity, such as wheat cooked in water, and rice, and testes, and pies, and belly of calf, and eggs, and fresh fish, and strong wine, and similar things. For with such nourishment they recover strength and the fracture heals more quickly.
And know that bones broken in the elderly and decrepit people cannot be well set or healed according to their nature because of dryness of the limbs, nor those who are too soft, such as the bones of small children.
But if there is no cure, put the broken bone back into the big tendons that are tight and join the limb together, as much as it can until it is strong as before. and to all points of his work, and for this reason he should be fed with thick meats, as we have said.
And know that fractures are diverse in many ways.
For the fracture of the head is different from the fracture of the leg or hip, and I will tell you in this chapter all kinds of fractures. For there is a fracture that goes into a groove, and another in sharpening, like the edge of a pen for writing.
For there is a fracture that goes into a groove, and another in sharpening, like the edge of a pen for writing. And if with fracture and wound, and fracture to a small crack all around.
And to each manner has its own instrument and tool proper in the restoration, according to what comes to mind in its place.
And it is known the fracture of the bone by those that the skin is twisted, and opens well outward, and sounds upon pressure of the hand.
and when there is no twisting or deformity at the touch, and the patient feels no great pain, there is no fracture.
but it can still have twisting, or a split, or a small fracture. If you must not move it by hand, but put on the medicines that come after here, and then restrain it subtly.

Then restrain it, as it should be hereafter.
And if the bone is broken into pieces, if it is of two parts, whether hand or foot, or to your hand if it is one small limb, or two cords, or one cord and to your hand;
And put the limb according to its natural figure, so that when the parts of the broken limb are separated, you move by all means the additions in their places as much as you can, and keep that through your work no damage or pain comes to the patient.
And adjust well the two heads of the bones according to the best forms.
And feel it well with your hand if it seems to you that it is not well.
If it is well aligned, and do not restrain it too much, nor press as those who make come from their work abscesses hot and contractions to the limb, as we have wanted many times.
And when the limb is in place, carefully instruct the patient that he does not move it, neither while awake nor sleeping, nor turning, nor in any other way; And guard well that the limb be so secure that it feels no pain at all. For when it feels no pain, it is not in place, and it is necessary to put it in such a position that it feels no pain.
And guard well that the limb be so secure that it feels no pain at all. For when it feels no pain, it is not in place, and it is necessary to put it in such a position that it feels no pain. And guard that it be set straight, that no twisting may come when it is restored.
And the qualities of the broken limb are according to what I tell you.
Know that broken limbs are diverse in smallness and in greatness and in form.
For there are small limbs, such as arms and legs and similar things: so it is necessary to envelop them with soft and fine linen cloths; and those that are large, such as hips and thighs:
so it is necessary to envelop them with a wide and firm linen, because the broad linens enclose the whole large limb, and restrain it from all sides equally, so that there is no gap;
Then bind with light bandages, III or IV turns according to the limb, and hold your hand a little on all the linen, then go above the fracture and restrain it less, that the fracture then move away a little from the fracture;
And soften the linen a little at a time, as long as you feel most of the healthy part, then take the other bandage, and turn it back over the broken part;
Then go back to the part below the fracture, and restrain it moderately, neither too tightly nor too loosely, as we have said in the first hour above;
Then put between the bandages padding of linen or cloths, as long as it is all equal, then tie everything above with the bandage, and then put your well-made splints on top, if there is no abscess or swelling.
And if there is a swelling or abscess, put on it a soothing thing for the abscess hot, and which removes the swelling, and let him thus for some days.
Then restrain above the edges, and let them be half a cane's breadth carefully drawn, or of wood from which one makes clips, or of palm branches, and let them be according to this figure:
But it is necessary that the edge that one puts on the fracture be a little bigger and wider than the others;
And let it also be as long as it is necessary for the limb of smallness or greatness; Then restrain on the edges also as you did in the first hour, then tie above a thread evenly, as in the first hour on the fracture; More strict is above, less below;
And it is necessary that the thread be medium, neither too thick nor too fine, and be of soft linen.
For if the thread is thick, as I want works of fools who make their stitches with coarse linen cords, and it is a great error, because the stitch cannot be equal if it is too fine, and it should not be that between each star there is less than two points of space.
And if the patient is sore at the sites of the stitches after the tightening in their healthy parts, put under them poultices of linen, or wool felt, so that it does not become blistered.
When the wound is with a fracture, and the bone is broken, I will say its cure here after.
And know that it does not behoove to straighten all the broken limbs on the first day. Because when the member is large, it is not necessary to put stitches further than after 5 or 7 days, or more according to your surety of abscess heat appearing.
And see here the manner of ancient plasters for restoring fracture, dislocation, and torsion:
And it is that you take flour that is held to the walls of the mill, and mix it with abundance of egg, and put this preparation, neither too clear nor too thick, and in use, and this is the common manner of the plaster restoring the fracture to many dispositions, and also to women and to small children, because it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Another manner of plaster restoring the fracture and dislocation and the twist: Take bayberry and lead, and mule, and asafoetida, and gum arabic, and mugwort, an ounce ten. myrrh and aloes, an ounce five. and alechil nine ounces. and make it powder, and mix them with yolk of an egg; if the complexion of the man is hot, he should use this plaster, because it is of good composition, and restores broken bones completely, and is worth to many men.
Another manner of plaster that is good for twist: take mugwort and wormwood, an ounce ten. myrrh and aloes, an ounce nine. and acai six ounces. Bolic armenic X Z., and grind well, and confit with water or yolk of an egg, and administer it.
Another manner to plaster joints and bones dislocated from their places, and appease the pain arising, and the usage of bone, and the fracture: Take new wool, and moisten it in oil and heat, and put it upon the place.
This plaster does not restore, but it is good for soothing a hot abscess, and removes pain properly.
Another manner to plaster which restores broken bones: Take dried fig leaves, and earth walnut leaves, and grind together, and make a plaster.
Another manner of emplastering for the restoration of a broken bone and to lessen the remainder of the abscess. Take root of mallow, chamomile, flowers of violets, flour of quince, of each one part;
And grind, and confit with wine, and if the limb is withered, confit it with coriander water, or water, and use it.
Another way of setting a bone more forcefully than the first, when a hard abscess comes to the restoration of the limb. Take roots of mallow, and seeds of flax, borage, melilot, and wormwood, and flowers of violets and chamomile, of all one part. and grind it, and confit with water of salt, or sweet water, or with wine, according to the heat of the limb, and its rest.
Ipocras says in his book that one should not put on the broken limb for restoration other than a certain one made of wax and oil, and nothing else more, and he says that it should be between two, neither too soft nor too hard.
And it seems Galen one must put on the broken member, to restore, things dry and a little warm, such as myrrh and aloe and incense and similar things, as we have said.
Another manner of ointment that is good for the lesion and pain: take mugwort and heads of doves cut off, or feathers of bird, and mallow, and wool equally, and grind them, and make an ointment from it and bind it.
And then unbind it a little afterwards, and take care if the patient feels no pain, or if his bones have not moved from their place; if not unbind it not for many days. And if pain comes to the place, or itching, no rest or swelling, then hasten, and untie it, and remove the plaster now.
And take a light cloth, and sponge of moist sea, and dip it in water, and lift the place up, as long as the itching ceases, and the pain is appeased. Then let the limb rest for an hour, then put on a dry cloth, moistened in water or rose oil, and tie it with a bandage, until you are sure of the hot abscess;
And soothe the inflammation of the limb, and the pain is removed, then begin the delicate bandage again, and a little plaster, and do not bind it as tightly as before; and gently until he is healed.
And if you see that the abscess is hot and red, and the pain and swelling are all gone, and you need emplastrum and binding, then begin it again, just as you did at first.
And if it does not come to the limb as we have said, do not unbind it except after III days or IV or V or VII. and no time leave it for XX days, according to what we see, until the fracture is joined, and approaches the setting of the bone.
Therefore, restrain it more than before, and make its flesh thicker, as I said earlier.
And if you see the place drying more than is necessary, know that there cannot come any nourishment; Therefore, use hot water, every time you untie it, and every third day, and moisten the place a little.
Through this work then nourish the limb, and heal quickly those who do foolish things, that they return the limb twisted;
This is bad, and this work is dangerous, and I see nothing else than to let it be and work it straight.
There are many ways of fractures, and diverse shapes, and the causes are destined;
for there are fractures that are made by the percussion of a sword, and the collarbone all the bone up to the surface which is below the bone, as if made high or low; and for this reason this kind of fracture is called “haichure.”
When the sword cuts through the bone from above without piercing it, and this kind of fracture is called “absolue”;
and is the wound of these two fractures large or small.
And if there is an enmeshed percussion, or a split or of the bone's neck, or of the head on a bone, or similar things.
And if it has a fracture penetrating, which approaches up to the skin that is below the bone, or it is above the bone, and this wound is great or small.
And if the wound which is put back into the bone as subtly as a hair, and is the fissure small, and for this reason this fracture is called “capillaire”;
and if the wound which is made either by hitting with stone, or by falling, or by similar thing, and makes the piece of bone enter inside, and is the  piece like how smiths do when they hit on cauldrons; if it goes between the pieces inside.
And this happens more to those who have or whose head is moist bone, like that of a child.
and it happens in all these ways of fracture where small pieces are dislodged as if stars; and I will speak of them in their place.
And let us know all the ways of fractures by retracting upon them, and by probing with probes, and by the corrupted flesh that is on it.
The seeming capillary is known by this: when one has uncovered the bone, and rubs upon it, the fracture appears black.
In the cure of a fracture, take care of the accidents of the patient, and if you see no bad accident, such as vomiting of bile, and loss of understanding, and extension, and paleness, and fever ague, and eyes becoming inflamed and red, and other similar things in more dispositions, then it cannot heal without doubt.
And if you do not see an escapable accident, and you hope for salvation, so commence the cure.
And when the fracture comes to you first, and it is in winter, then it is necessary to remove the bone before the fourteenth day as much as you can.
And if it is in summer, then hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, so that the tissue under the bone does not rot, and comes the accident that we have said.
And if the fracture of the bone comes to the membrane that covers the brain, then you should split the fractured part like this:
and that you press the head of the patient down firmly, and uncover it on the bone in all ways that you can, and on the figure of the wound more carefully than you can, and if bleeding comes when uncovering the bone, or abscess put a bandage on it, if it is needed, and that you fill the place with cloths moistened in rose oil; and make the wound rest until the abscess is healed, and be assured of bleeding cessation; Then begins to remove the bone, and it is done according to two ways of work:
One way is that you cut at the narrow edge in front, and this is the form:
And then put another incision larger than the first one.
And the second manner is that you have a number of different cuts, some narrow and others wide, and some pointed, and some blunt;
and in work lightly, so that you do not break the head.
And if the bone is hard and stiff, then it behooves you to pierce it around, so that you administer cuts with chisels, no more deeply than that it does not go through the test of bone, and it must have above the point a margin or circle that does not allow the point to pass beyond the bone;
And it behooves you to have several, so that each comes in its place of the thickness of the bone, so that you have chisels for all tests;
and this is the form of the III manners of bandages:
The qualities to perceive around the broken bone is that you place a bandage on the bone, and turn to your two until you know that the bone is pierced.
then place the bandage elsewhere a little after, and thus do all around, then cut with the incisors the space of the bone that is between the perforations, and do these more subtly than you can, and lightly, as we have said, until you draw the bone to your hand, or to any of the instruments that you have such as pliers, pincers;
and keep that you do not touch the brain nor the spinal cord.
And when you will have the bone pierced, and treated, it is fitting that you reset the apparatus which is detached from the bone to all other instruments similar to an incisor, but it is fitting that it be more tenacious and more subtle than the other incisor.
and if it remains any of the small bones, or no scales, so carefully to the bone with instruments. then cure the wound with tents and dressings as we have said.
And the other manner of work is that which Galien says, which is drier. and the load much strong, and that is his word. It is necessary first that you uncover a part of the bone, where the fracture appears to move, so that when you have uncovered that part, then place under the head of this lentil-shaped incisional instrument, for this is its form:
And do not trench from one side, and is the party acute in II coasts, so that the trenching is towards the bone;
then strike with a small hammer above, so that you tap lightly the bone all around, and give yourself well guard of the cloth that is underneath;
if it is adhered to the bone, then gently remove it, because it is well removed without annoyance, and without fear.
And if the bone fracture does not reach the skin, and the bone is broken only from above, and there are small pieces, it is necessary to first apply this asperity, and remove the scales with a fine rasp which you must have in various quantities, to administer in each place the most suitable one, according to the work and the figure of the fracture.
If you begin with coarse rasps, and afterwards with finer ones, and do not cease doing this until it is well.
In each of the other capillary cuts and subtle fracture, you must bring cause according to its arrangement;
and that is thing which is not replied to him who has put his study in this art.
And if the wound is uncovered, it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and moisten it in rose oil, and place it on the wound. then take another cloth doubled in two or three, and moisten it in wine and rose oil, and place it on the first cloth; and do this more gently than you can, so that you do not injure the wound, then administer above it another linen cloth, and do not bind it too tightly except to keep the wound closed;
and leave it thus for one or two days, until the abscess is calmed, then untie it, and administer medicines such as roots of licorice, and chickpea flour, and frankincense powder, and arnica, and similar things.
and from these things make a powder, and put it on the wound, and administer other medicinal ointments without biting.
and guard that the wound be quite clean, that you do not leave there any ointment, nor let it join together with flesh, because when flesh joins on the brain's membrane, it inflames and hurts, and brings great evil to the patient;
because when one puts upon it a biting thing, it is burned from above. Take ward then, if no bad accidents come to the patient, as we have said, so it is doubtful without doubt.
And if blacking does not come forth except for the medicine that has been put upon it, and if it has virtue, then it behooves you to take one part of honey, and three parts of rose oil and in glue one drape, and place it upon the wound. then treat it with the ways of treatment that are proper until it is healed.
Know that at times the two parts of the nose are broken above, or one by itself, because they are two bones;
and this below is cartilaginous, and does not break, unless it is forced to bend or twist, and then it heals.
And if one of the two parts is broken, then you must put your small finger or the other into it, and place that fracture inside your index finger, and on the palm outward, until the bone returns according to its natural shape. and do this lightly, and guard so that the patient does not feel pain from your work.
And if the fracture is in the part above the nose, and it cannot come to its place, then you should put it even on top of a rail a little thick.
And if the fracture is of two parts, do this again, and hasten to restore it on the first day, if you can.
If not, after the seventh or tenth day, when the abscess has subsided, then put on the head of the finger bandages of linen, if the fracture is in one part.
Or you break the other side if there is a fracture there; and let the tents be so large that they fill the openings of the wounds.
And they said any restorer one must moisten the tents in butter, and change each day. It does not seem to me that this is good, but it is necessary that they be moistened in the preparation of a mixture of egg yolk and ground pepper. Then leave the tent until the bone is hard and the cartilage is hardened.
And at no time should one put goose feathers in their place, and if the wind blows through there, and it holds the fracture, and it causes the patient to blow among them; or do what you see fit.

And if an abscess arises, apply a poultice from the outside of ground sesame seed and powdered incense, mixed with egg yolk, then put light bandages on top and do not bind the wounds tightly;
And if the fracture of the bone is small, and it is broken into small pieces, then extract them with suitable instruments, then stitch the wound, and care for it with appropriate things like flesh, and bandage, as with plasters and ointments suitable for that;
And if inside the bone there is no wound, then cure it with bandages, and put in some splints, until it is healed.
When the jaw is broken below, and there is no external wound, and the fracture is only outside, and is not broken in two, except for a bulge inside, if one knows it lightly.
It is necessary, if the fracture is in the right part, that you put your index finger into the mouth of the patient. And if the fracture is in the left jawbone, then put your right index finger into the mouth, and gently push the fracture inwards, and hold it with the other hand from outside, and make it even.
And if the jawbone fracture is broken in two, it is necessary that you administer an extension of two parts, until it is straight.
And if movement of the teeth happens, or a rupture, then restrain them with a thread of gold or silver or silk, then put on top of the broken jawbone a cerot, and put a double cloth over it; And place on the cloth a large plate well made, or a piece of leather even, long and wide according to the jawbone, and tie it onto the wound as much as you can, and command the patient to rest, and eat light meats.
And if you see that the figure changes, then untie it on the third day, and then put back in place what has changed. And place a plaster on top, of the powder from the mill with an abundance of egg or sesame flour, after what you have removed the cautery.
And place a light linen bandage on top, and if the plasters dry, and the patient's condition does not change, leave him as long as he is healthy. Many times it heals in three weeks.
And if an abscess comes, administer what we have said many times.

And if the wound is narrow, then enlarge it with flame according to your need.
And when you will have removed all these pieces, and there is none remaining, then cover the wound, if it is wide, and put upon it one plaster suitable to that, in order to hinder flesh, until it is healed.
The fracture breaks more often in front, towards the shoulder, and the fracture is according to three manners:
Where it is broken and separated in two, and there are pieces, and it is the lightest to restore;
Or there is a fracture with a wound.
And the work of this is when the fracture is without a wound, that you have two ministers, so that one holds the arm that feels the broken bone; and the other extends the neck towards the part;
then put the fracture equal to your two, so that the form is as it should be, and there be no appearance or gap.

and treat the pieces lightly, and if the bone cannot join by any raising, then it cut with one of the incisions that you have. but that you place under the force a this instrument, to keep the soft tissue.
and resembles a spoon, and is not quite hollowed out, and be as wide as it suits, according to the size of the bone and the strength of the work.
and it has two ends, as you see, and one be wide, and the other narrow, as is the break of the wound that you made when you treated the bones;

The tightening of the bone, when it is without wounds and without cutting, is that you put on the bone the plaster of powder from the mill and beaten egg white, and put light dressings above, and put a sphere under the seat, if you need it. Then take a very wide bandage of one palm width, then put the double cloth above the dressings and the plaster. Then take a slender stick of three fingers wide and long, and wrap it around that bandaged one, so that the head of the bandage hangs down. and put the stick on the bruise, and turn the bandage around the neck, and under the healthy armpit, and under the affected armpit; and tighten it on each side, and when you see that the bandage holds the bruise well, and you clearly see that the stick does not move above the broken bone, then reward (bind) it every day, and when the bandage loosens, then bind it again;
and make the patient lie on his back, and put a small pillow under the armpit when he sleeps, in order to raise the shoulder higher; so it will lighten the bruise from the pain by aligning the shoulder, or you bind his arm to his neck, and do not unbind it unless a bad accident happens, until twenty days;
then restart the bandaging if you see that it is necessary, and restrain it and leave it like this until it is restored
and it is restored within at most XXVIII days, and in less for no man
of the fracture of the shoulder
It happens sometimes that the shoulder breaks or its socket is displaced in the middle, and it is not known if there was a fracture except by touch;
so set the joint according to the figure of the fracture, and reset it according to its natural form, in every way that you can;
then put on top of the wound powder from the mill with an abundance of egg, and light dressings, and put a double cloth above; then put on top a wide and thin pad, a bit wider than the shoulder, and if any part of the pad touches the shoulder, then place cotton dressings beneath it so that the pad lies evenly;
and then binds it with a very strong bandage firmly so that the bone does not move from its place, and look at the joint every day.
and every time it softens, binds it again, and places the bone straight if it is out of place, and lies the patient on his side.
and the shoulder is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five.
And when this number is complete, then untie it, and be sure, because it is not bone that is easily put out of place.
And if a piece of bone protrudes forward, and not below the joint, so do it above, and the bone, and do what we have said in the fracture of the collarbone to soothe the hot abscess.
of the fracture of the leg
The bone is sometimes broken in the middle, but this happens rarely; but it often breaks at the edge.
And when it is broken in its marrow, it sinks to the lower part, and the patient feels great pain, and lack of strength, and all things, and perhaps vomits blood, and opens the passages within the broken bone, and it smells quite bad.
And the restoration is that the patient lies often on his back, and puts one cushion between the shoulders, then presses the shoulders together, and joins the ribs of the two ribs with your hands, and carefully tries to put them straight in every way as you can, until the figure of the bone is as it should be;
then put on plasters and bandages over the thin layer of salt, or other light wool, and wrap it in a cloth, and lay it on top of the broken bone so that it doesn't move and turn the band around to the back, and restrain it firmly, and reward each hour the linen, and if it softens, restrain it;
and if it is necessary to loosen it, for corrosion or for itching that comes to the place, or pain or abscess, then hurry and loosen it, and scrape away the plaster, and address what has come up there with methods of healing;
then put back on the plaster, and restrain it until it is healed.
of the breakage of the ribs
Know that the fracture of the ribs comes only in their thick parts after the back, and at the head within, and they bend only for that reason that they are glassy; and one feels it well at the inquiry so does.
And the restoration is that you set the fracture equal, as it is right. And then put the splint on it, and restrain it with a stick, if you need it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is declining inwards, one feels the patient great pain, and prickling, as well as from pleurisy, because the bone points the skin. and besides pains, and coughs and vomits great planty of blood, and this is of grave sickness, and the ancients put great engine upon it.
And if some say it is necessary to administer meats that swell the belly, to expel the wind outward.
We do not do this, except for hot abscesses, if they have not yet come. And if it has come, then treat it.
And they said of these [ventouses], one puts on the place to suck strongly, and this seems reasonable. Nevertheless, there is doubt that the suction does not draw the superfluities to the place for the disposition of weakness.
And they say any one of you should cover the place with a woolen cloth in hot oil, and put tents between the ribs to make equal the lying wound, then be cured of the plague of pleurisy, meat, and medicine.
And if the patient feels great pain that he cannot bear because of the bone that pokes the skin, and there is doubt for the patient, then it is necessary to split above the place of the broken rib, and it is necessary to put underneath an instrument to ward off the lung.
And then one must cut the bone, and drain slightly, and then sew the two lips of the wound, if it is large, and cure it with ointments until it is healed.
And if an abscess comes, then put on a soft cloth in rose oil, and put it on the place.
and cure while the place of those that it is fitting to abscess, and be lying soundly on the ribs where it should, until it is healed.
and also to the spine of the back.
And when this happens to someone, and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then take note if you see his hands softened and swollen and dead, and he cannot move them nor extend them, and when you prick them with a needle, he feels nothing.
Know that it is mortal in many dispositions, and if they move and feel well when they are pulled and held still, know that the joint of the bone is saved, and that the patient will be cured if one puts care to it.

of the break of the bone of the hip

And if the fracture is at the top only, set it straight, as long as it is in natural form.

of the fracture of the shoulder
The shoulder is that which is between the rib and the head of the shoulder, and if it is broken, one must restore it according to one of two ways.
one of which is that you take a piece of medium thick wood, according to this form:
And tie the two pieces with two bands, and bend them upward, and set the patient on a stool.
